Set up:

Let r = radius Ω, R = radius Γ, with r < R. They intersect. So d > R - r and d < R + r.

Define:

t = (r^2 - R^2) / (2d). This is negative.
Let u = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d). Then u > 0. Also note that t + d/2 = u. Indeed: t + d/2 = (r^2 - R^2)/(2d) + d/2 = (r^2 - R^2 + d^2) / (2d) = u.

Also t - d/2 = (r^2 - R^2)/(2d) - d/2 = (r^2 - R^2 - d^2) / (2d) = -(d^2 + R^2 - r^2) / (2d) = -v, where v = (d^2 + R^2 - r^2) / (2d) > 0.

We have a_y = sqrt(r^2 - u^2). Also a_y = sqrt(R^2 - v^2). So both are defined.

Now C = (-d/2 - r, 0). D = (d/2 + R, 0).

Now P is circumcenter of ACD. Let's compute P. Since C and D are on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line at x = (C_x + D_x)/2 = ((-d/2 - r) + (d/2 + R)) / 2 = (R - r)/2. So P_x = (R - r)/2. Good.

Now we need P_y. Let's denote P = (X, Y) with X = (R - r)/2.

We have PA^2 = PC^2.

PC^2 = (X - C_x)^2 + Y^2 = (X - (-d/2 - r))^2 + Y^2 = (X + d/2 + r)^2 + Y^2.

Compute X + d/2 + r = (R - r)/2 + d/2 + r = (R - r + d + 2r)/2 = (R + d + r)/2.

So (X - C_x)^2 = ((R + d + r)/2)^2.

Thus PC^2 = ((R + d + r)^2)/4 + Y^2.

Similarly, PA^2 = (t - X)^2 + (a_y - Y)^2.

Set equal:

(t - X)^2 + (a_y - Y)^2 = ((R + d + r)^2)/4 + Y^2.

Expand (a_y - Y)^2 = a_y^2 - 2 a_y Y + Y^2. Then equation becomes:

(t - X)^2 + a_y^2 - 2 a_y Y + Y^2 = ((R + d + r)^2)/4 + Y^2.

Cancel Y^2. So:

(t - X)^2 + a_y^2 - 2 a_y Y = ((R + d + r)^2)/4.

Thus:

2 a_y Y = (t - X)^2 + a_y^2 - ((R + d + r)^2)/4.

So Y = [ (t - X)^2 + a_y^2 - ((R + d + r)^2)/4 ] / (2 a_y).

Now we can try to simplify this expression. Compute (t - X). t = (r^2 - R^2)/(2d). X = (R - r)/2. So

t - X = (r^2 - R^2)/(2d) - (R - r)/2 = ( (r^2 - R^2) - d(R - r) ) / (2d). As before, = -(R - r)(R + r + d) / (2d). So (t - X)^2 = (R - r)^2 (R + r + d)^2 / (4 d^2).

Now a_y^2 = r^2 - u^2, where u = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d). So a_y^2 = r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2/(4 d^2). That's an expression.

We also have ((R + d + r)^2)/4.

We need to simplify Y. The expression seems messy. However perhaps Y simplifies nicely to something like ( (R - r) * (something) ) / (2 a_y). Let's compute Y explicitly with symbolic simplification. Let’s define:

S3 = ((R + d + r)^2)/4.

Then Y = (S1 + S2 - S3) / (2 a_y).

Now (X - t) = (R - r)/2 - (r^2 - R^2)/(2d). Simplify as before: (R - r)/2 + (R^2 - r^2)/(2d) = (R - r)/2 + (R - r)(R + r)/(2d) = (R - r) [ 1/2 + (R + r)/(2d) ] = (R - r) (d + R + r) / (2d). So (X - t)^2 = (R - r)^2 S^2 / (4 d^2). Good.

Now also a^2 = r^2 - (t + d/2)^2. But maybe we can express a^2 in terms of (R - r)^2 and something. Let's compute (t + d/2)^2 = ( (r^2 - R^2)/(2d) + d/2 )^2 = ( (r^2 - R^2 + d^2) / (2d) )^2. So u = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d). So a^2 = r^2 - u^2.

Now plug into Y expression.

Y = [ (X - t)^2 + a^2 - (X - c1)^2 ] / (2a). So

Y = \frac{ \frac{(R - r)^2 S^2}{4 d^2} + r^2 - \frac{S^2}{4} - \frac{ (R - r)^2 S^2? Actually careful: (X - c1)^2 = S^2/4. So the numerator of Y (call NumY) is:

NumY = \frac{(R - r)^2 S^2}{4 d^2} + a^2 - \frac{S^2}{4}.

Then Y = NumY / (2a). So Y = \frac{ \frac{(R - r)^2 S^2}{4 d^2} + a^2 - \frac{S^2}{4} }{2a}.

Now we need to simplify a^2. a^2 = r^2 - u^2, with u = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d). So

a^2 = r^2 - \frac{ (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2 }{4 d^2}.

Thus

NumY = \frac{ (R - r)^2 S^2 }{4 d^2} + r^2 - \frac{ (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2 }{4 d^2} - \frac{S^2}{4}.

Combine terms over 4 d^2:

NumY = \frac{ (R - r)^2 S^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2 }{4 d^2} + \frac{4 r^2 - S^2}{4}.

Now combine as before:

Thus Y = \frac{ (R - r)^2 S^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2 + 4 d^2 r^2 - d^2 S^2 }{8 a d^2}.

Simplify: = \frac{1}{2} \left( R - 1 + \frac{R^2 - 1}{d} \right). Since (R^2 - 1) = (R-1)(R+1). So

Maybe we can use coordinates but also use vector algebra to avoid messy expansions. Let's try to compute Y in a more systematic way.

We have M = (0,0), N = (d,0). Let r = 1, R > 1.

A = (x0, a) with x0 = (d^2 + 1 - R^2)/(2d), a^2 = 1 - x0^2. (1)

Now compute (X - x0) and (X + 1). Let's compute X - x0:

X - x0 = \frac{d + R - 1}{2} - \frac{d^2 + 1 - R^2}{2d} = \frac{1}{2} \left( d + R - 1 - \frac{d^2 + 1 - R^2}{d} \right). Simplify fraction:

\frac{d^2 + 1 - R^2}{d} = d + \frac{1 - R^2}{d}. So

X - x0 = \frac{1}{2} \left( d + R - 1 - d - \frac{1 - R^2}{d} \right) = \frac{1}{2} \left( R - 1 - \frac{1 - R^2}{d} \right) = \frac{1}{2} \left( R - 1 - \frac{1}{d} + \frac{R^2}{d} \right) = \frac{1}{2} \left( R - 1 + \frac{R^2 - 1}{d} \right). So

X - x0 = \frac{1}{2} \left( R - 1 + \frac{(R-1)(R+1)}{d} \right) = \frac{R-1}{2} \left( 1 + \frac{R+1}{d} \right). Good.

Now (X + 1) = \frac{d + R - 1}{2} + 1 = \frac{d + R - 1 + 2}{2} = \frac{d + R + 1}{2}.

Now also a^2 = 1 - x0^2.

Now compute Y. I'll denote Δ = (X - x0)^2 + a^2 - (X + 1)^2. Then Y = Δ / (2a). So

Y = \frac{ (X - x0)^2 + a^2 - (X + 1)^2 }{2a}.

We need to simplify Δ. Let's compute (X - x0)^2 - (X + 1)^2. That's difference of squares: ( (X - x0) - (X + 1) ) * ( (X - x0) + (X + 1) ) = ( -x0 - 1 ) * ( 2X - x0 + 1 )? Actually:

(X - x0) - (X + 1) = -x0 - 1.
(X - x0) + (X + 1) = 2X - x0 + 1.

So (X - x0)^2 - (X + 1)^2 = (-x0 - 1)(2X - x0 + 1). But we also have a^2 term. So Δ = (X - x0)^2 - (X + 1)^2 + a^2.

Thus Δ = a^2 + (X - x0)^2 - (X + 1)^2. But we can also attempt to express a^2 in terms of x0 and maybe relate to something.

We have a^2 = 1 - x0^2. So

Δ = 1 - x0^2 + (X - x0)^2 - (X + 1)^2.

Now expand (X - x0)^2 = X^2 - 2 X x0 + x0^2. Then

Δ = 1 - x0^2 + X^2 - 2 X x0 + x0^2 - (X^2 + 2X + 1) = 1 + X^2 - 2 X x0 - X^2 - 2X - 1 = -2 X x0 - 2X = -2X (x0 + 1). So the X^2 terms cancel, and 1 cancels. Good! That's a simplification.

Let's verify carefully:

Δ = 1 - x0^2 + (X - x0)^2 - (X + 1)^2.

Compute (X - x0)^2 = X^2 - 2 X x0 + x0^2.
Compute (X + 1)^2 = X^2 + 2X + 1.
Then (X - x0)^2 - (X + 1)^2 = (X^2 - 2 X x0 + x0^2) - (X^2 + 2X + 1) = -2 X x0 + x0^2 - 2X - 1.
Then Δ = 1 - x0^2 + (-2 X x0 + x0^2 - 2X - 1) = 1 - x0^2 -2 X x0 + x0^2 - 2X - 1 = -2 X x0 - 2X = -2X (x0 + 1).

So indeed Δ = -2 X (x0 + 1). That's neat. Then Y = Δ / (2a) = - X (x0 + 1) / a.

Thus we have an expression for Y in terms of X, x0, a. Since a > 0, and X > 0 likely (since d+R-1 > 0). So Y is negative if X(x0+1) > 0? But x0 could be negative (since d^2+1-R^2 may be negative). Let's examine sign. In our numeric with r=1,d=2,R=4? Actually we need to test. But anyway Y is something.

Now we also have H. M = (0,0), N = (d,0), P = (X, Y). Then H = (X, y_H) with y_H = X(d - X) / Y (from earlier). Because (H - M) ⋅ (P - N) = 0 → X*(X - d) + y_H Y = 0 → X(X - d) + y_H Y = 0 → y_H Y = -X(X - d) = X(d - X). So y_H = X(d - X) / Y.

Thus H = (X, X(d - X) / Y).

Now AP direction vector v = P - A = (X - x0, Y - a). We'll need to find E and F.

We have A = (x0, a). Let’s param: A + t * v. Then at t = 0, A; at t = t_E, point is E on Ω; at t = t_F, point is F on Γ. Since A is on both circles, the second intersection corresponds to t = t_E and t = t_F respectively, where t_E and t_F are the other roots of quadratic. We can find t_E by solving (A + t v) ∈ Ω. Since A is on Ω, we have:

| (A + t v) - M |^2 = r^2 = 1. Since M = (0,0). So |A + t v|^2 = 1. But |A|^2 = 1 because A ∈ Ω. So

|A + t v|^2 = |A|^2 + 2 t (A·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = 1 + 2 t (A·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = 1.

Thus 2 t (A·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 → t (2 (A·v) + t |v|^2) = 0. So t=0 (A) or t = -2 (A·v) / |v|^2. So t_E = -2 (A·v) / |v|^2. Similarly, for Γ with center N = (d,0), radius R. The equation: | (A + t v) - N |^2 = R^2. Since A ∈ Γ, |A - N|^2 = R^2. So

|A - N + t v|^2 = |A - N|^2 + 2 t ((A - N)·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = R^2 + 2 t ((A - N)·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = R^2. Then 2 t ((A - N)·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 → t (2 ((A - N)·v) + t |v|^2) = 0. So t=0 (A) or t_F = -2 ((A - N)·v) / |v|^2.

Thus we have E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v.

Given the simplification that Y = - X (x0+1) / a, and H = (X, X(d - X) / Y). Since Y appears in denominator, we can compute y_H. Plug Y expression.

Y = - X (x0+1) / a. So

Similarly, t_F = -2 Re((A - N) \overline{v}) / |v|^2. Let's compute Re((A - N) \overline{v}) in terms of c,s,d,X,Y.

We have A - N = (c - d, s). Then \overline{v} = (X - c) - i (Y - s). So (A - N) \overline{v} = ((c - d) + i s) ((X - c) - i (Y - s)). Expand:

Real part = (c - d)*(X - c) + s*(Y - s). (since i * -i = +1 for the term i s * (-i (Y - s)) = s (Y - s).) Good.

Now s*(Y - s) = sY - s^2. But Y is expressed. So we can compute.

We have Re((A - N) \overline{v}) = (c - d)*(X - c) + sY - s^2.

Now also note that (c - d)*(X - c) = (c - d)X - (c - d)c = (c - d)X - c^2 + cd.

Thus Re((A - N) \overline{v}) = (c - d)X - c^2 + cd + sY - s^2.

Now c^2 + s^2 = 1, so -c^2 - s^2 = -1. But we have -c^2 + cd + sY - s^2 = -(c^2 + s^2) + cd + sY = -1 + cd + sY.

Thus Re((A - N) \overline{v}) = (c - d)X - 1 + cd + sY.

Now combine with the X term. So

Re((A - N) \overline{v}) = (c - d)X + cd + sY - 1.

Now we also have an expression for sY: Y = - X (c+1) / s, so sY = -X (c+1). Then

Re((A - N) \overline{v}) = (c - d)X + cd - X (c+1) - 1 = (c - d)X - X(c+1) + cd - 1 = X(c - d - c - 1) + cd - 1 = X(-d - 1) + cd - 1 = -X(d+1) + cd - 1.

That's what we had. Good.

Now also compute |v|^2 = (X - c)^2 + (Y - s)^2. Expand:

|v|^2 = (X - c)^2 + (Y - s)^2 = (X^2 - 2cX + c^2) + (Y^2 - 2sY + s^2).

We know c^2 + s^2 = 1. So

|v|^2 = X^2 + Y^2 - 2(cX + sY) + 1.

Now cX + sY =? We can compute cX + sY. Since Y is expressed, sY = -X(c+1). So cX + sY = cX - X(c+1) = X(c - c - 1) = -X. So cX + sY = -X. Then

|v|^2 = X^2 + Y^2 - 2(-X) + 1 = X^2 + Y^2 + 2X + 1.

But also note that X^2 + Y^2 is |P|^2. That's something.

Now also we can compute (X+1) appears. So t_E = 2 (X+1) / |v|^2.

Now we have E = A + t_E v. So E = (c + t_E (X - c), s + t_E (Y - s)). Similarly F.

Let's try a different coordinate: Place MN as the x-axis, but also place the common chord AB as the y-axis (vertical). That is natural: Let AB be vertical. Then MN is horizontal. The midpoint O of AB lies on MN, so O is a point on x-axis. So we can set O = (t, 0). Then M = ( -a, 0) and N = (b, 0) with a > 0, b > 0, and t is some value (could be negative or positive). M = (-a, 0), N = (b, 0). Then MA^2 = (t + a)^2 + s^2 = r^2. NA^2 = (t - b)^2 + s^2 = R^2.

Also, the line MN (x-axis) intersects Ω at C and Γ at D with C, M, N, D in that order. Since M is at (-a,0) and N at (b,0), with a,b>0. The order C, M, N, D: C is left of M, D is right of N. Now, we also have A is an intersection point. We can param A as (x_A, y_A). Since A is on both circles, we have MA^2 = r^2, NA^2 = R^2. Also, A is not on MN. We can derive a relation between x_A and y_A.

Let’s denote:

M = (-d/2, 0), N = (d/2, 0). Then

MA^2 = (x_A + d/2)^2 + y_A^2 = r^2.
NA^2 = (x_A - d/2)^2 + y_A^2 = R^2.

Subtract: (x_A - d/2)^2 - (x_A + d/2)^2 = R^2 - r^2 → (x_A^2 - d x_A + d^2/4) - (x_A^2 + d x_A + d^2/4) = -2 d x_A = R^2 - r^2 → x_A = (r^2 - R^2) / (2d). Since r < R, x_A is negative. So A_x = (r^2 - R^2) / (2d). That's a negative value. Also, y_A^2 = r^2 - (x_A + d/2)^2.

Then vector P - N = (X - d/2, Y). N = (d/2, 0). The condition (H - M) · (P - N) = 0. Since H = (X, h). Then H - M = (X + d/2, h). Dot with (X - d/2, Y) = 0 → (X + d/2)*(X - d/2) + h Y = 0 → (X^2 - (d/2)^2) + h Y = 0 → h Y = (d^2/4) - X^2. So h = (d^2/4 - X^2) / Y, provided Y ≠ 0.

Let's test with numeric from symmetric coordinate: M = (-1,0), N = (1,0), r=1,R=√3. Then A = (-0.5, √3/2). X = (R - r)/2 = (√3 - 1)/2 ≈ 0.366. Then A·P = x_A X + y_A Y. x_A = -0.5, y_A ≈ 0.866. So that relation is specific to the coordinate where M=(0,0) and C = (-1,0). That seems to be a convenient choice.

R = 2X + 1 - d. Then square:

R^2 = (2X + 1 - d)^2 = 4X^2 + (1 - d)^2 + 4X(1 - d). That equals d^2 + 1 - 2cd. So

4X^2 + (1 - 2d + d^2) + 4X(1 - d) = d^2 + 1 - 2cd.

Simplify: 4X^2 + d^2 - 2d + 1 + 4X - 4Xd = d^2 + 1 - 2cd.

Cancel d^2 + 1 on both sides:

4X^2 - 2d + 4X - 4Xd = -2cd.

Divide 2: 2X^2 - d + 2X - 2Xd = -cd. So

2X^2 + 2X - d - 2Xd = -cd.

Combine terms with X: 2X^2 + 2X - 2Xd = 2X(X + 1 - d). So

2X(X + 1 - d) - d = -cd. => 2X(X + 1 - d) = d - cd = d(1 - c).

Thus

2X(X + 1 - d) = d(1 - c).   (6)

This is an interesting relation. Note that X is P_x. Also, we have Y = -X(c+1)/s. And we also have s^2 = 1 - c^2.

Now, maybe we can use this relation to simplify expressions for t_E, t_F, and the circle (BEF). Also, note that we have A·P = -X, which we already used. So we have some linear relations.

u = E - B = (c + t_E v_x - c, s + t_E v_y - (-s)) = (t_E v_x, 2s + t_E v_y). Similarly, v = (t_F v_x, 2s + t_F v_y).

Now, compute u^⊥. Since u = (t_E v_x, 2s + t_E v_y). A vector perpendicular to u is ( - (2s + t_E v_y), t_E v_x ) or ( (2s + t_E v_y), -t_E v_x ). So we can pick u^⊥ = ( - (2s + t_E v_y), t_E v_x ). That's perpendicular to u. Let's define:

p = ( - (2s + t_E v_y), t_E v_x ). Then O is some scalar multiple of p. So O = λ p.

Now, we also need O·v = k. Compute v = (t_F v_x, 2s + t_F v_y). Then O·v = λ (p·v). So λ = k / (p·v). So O = (k / (p·v)) p.

Now, we also have that O is the circumcenter, so |O - B| = r_BEF. But we already used that to get the equations. So we can compute O symbolically.

Now, we need to verify that L (through H parallel to v) is tangent to the circle (BEF). The tangency condition is:

| (O - H) × v | / |v| = |O - B|.

We have H = (X, h) with h = - s (d - X) / (c+1). That's from earlier. And v is known. O is expressed in terms of p, etc. This seems messy.

Let's attempt a coordinate approach.

Set up:

M = (-d/2, 0)
N = (d/2, 0)

Let r = radius Ω, R = radius Γ, with r < R. They intersect. So d > R - r and d < R + r.

Define:

t = (r^2 - R^2) / (2d). This is negative.
Let u = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d). Then u > 0. Also note that t + d/2 = u. Indeed: t + d/2 = (r^2 - R^2)/(2d) + d/2 = (r^2 - R^2 + d^2) / (2d) = u.

Also t - d/2 = (r^2 - R^2)/(2d) - d/2 = (r^2 - R^2 - d^2) / (2d) = -(d^2 + R^2 - r^2) / (2d) = -v, where v = (d^2 + R^2 - r^2) / (2d) > 0.

We have a_y = sqrt(r^2 - u^2). Also a_y = sqrt(R^2 - v^2). So both are defined.

Now C = (-d/2 - r, 0). D = (d/2 + R, 0).

Now P is circumcenter of ACD. Let's compute P. Since C and D are on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line at x = (C_x + D_x)/2 = ((-d/2 - r) + (d/2 + R)) / 2 = (R - r)/2. So P_x = (R - r)/2. Good.

Now we need P_y. Let's denote P = (X, Y) with X = (R - r)/2.

We have PA^2 = PC^2.

PC^2 = (X - C_x)^2 + Y^2 = (X - (-d/2 - r))^2 + Y^2 = (X + d/2 + r)^2 + Y^2.

Compute X + d/2 + r = (R - r)/2 + d/2 + r = (R - r + d + 2r)/2 = (R + d + r)/2.

So (X - C_x)^2 = ((R + d + r)/2)^2.

Thus PC^2 = ((R + d + r)^2)/4 + Y^2.

Similarly, PA^2 = (t - X)^2 + (a_y - Y)^2.

Set equal:

(t - X)^2 + (a_y - Y)^2 = ((R + d + r)^2)/4 + Y^2.

Expand (a_y - Y)^2 = a_y^2 - 2 a_y Y + Y^2. Then equation becomes:

(t - X)^2 + a_y^2 - 2 a_y Y + Y^2 = ((R + d + r)^2)/4 + Y^2.

Cancel Y^2. So:

(t - X)^2 + a_y^2 - 2 a_y Y = ((R + d + r)^2)/4.

Thus:

2 a_y Y = (t - X)^2 + a_y^2 - ((R + d + r)^2)/4.

So Y = [ (t - X)^2 + a_y^2 - ((R + d + r)^2)/4 ] / (2 a_y).

Now we can try to simplify this expression. Compute (t - X). t = (r^2 - R^2)/(2d). X = (R - r)/2. So

t - X = (r^2 - R^2)/(2d) - (R - r)/2 = ( (r^2 - R^2) - d(R - r) ) / (2d). As before, = -(R - r)(R + r + d) / (2d). So (t - X)^2 = (R - r)^2 (R + r + d)^2 / (4 d^2).

Now a_y^2 = r^2 - u^2, where u = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d). So a_y^2 = r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2/(4 d^2). That's an expression.

We also have ((R + d + r)^2)/4.

We need to simplify Y. The expression seems messy. However perhaps Y simplifies nicely to something like ( (R - r) * (something) ) / (2 a_y). Let's compute Y explicitly with symbolic simplification. Let’s define:

S1 = (t - X)^2 = (R - r)^2 (R + r + d)^2 / (4 d^2).
S2 = a_y^2 = r^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2 / (4 d^2).
S3 = ((R + d + r)^2)/4.

Then Y = (S1 + S2 - S3) / (2 a_y).

We need to see if S1 + S2 - S3 simplifies to something like (R - r) * something simple. Possibly it becomes (R - r) * (something like (d^2 - (R - r)^2) )? Or maybe it becomes (R - r) * (d^2 - (R - r)^2) / (2 d^2)? But we also have a_y factor. This looks messy.

We have P_x = X. So:

(X - x0)^2 + (Y - a)^2 = (X + 1)^2 + Y^2.

Expand (Y - a)^2 = Y^2 - 2aY + a^2. Cancel Y^2:

(X - x0)^2 + a^2 - 2aY = (X + 1)^2.

Thus

2aY = (X - x0)^2 + a^2 - (X + 1)^2.

So Y = \frac{ (X - x0)^2 + a^2 - (X + 1)^2 }{2a}.

Let’s compute X - x0:

Simplify: = \frac{1}{2} \left( R - 1 + \frac{R^2 - 1}{d} \right). Since (R^2 - 1) = (R-1)(R+1). So

Now (X + 1) = \frac{d + R - 1}{2} + 1 = \frac{d + R - 1 + 2}{2} = \frac{d + R + 1}{2}.

Now also a^2 = 1 - x0^2.

Now compute Y. I'll denote Δ = (X - x0)^2 + a^2 - (X + 1)^2. Then Y = Δ / (2a). So

Y = \frac{ (X - x0)^2 + a^2 - (X + 1)^2 }{2a}.

We need to simplify Δ. Let's compute (X - x0)^2 - (X + 1)^2. That's difference of squares: ( (X - x0) - (X + 1) ) * ( (X - x0) + (X + 1) ) = ( -x0 - 1 ) * ( 2X - x0 + 1 )? Actually:

(X - x0) - (X + 1) = -x0 - 1.
(X - x0) + (X + 1) = 2X - x0 + 1.

So (X - x0)^2 - (X + 1)^2 = (-x0 - 1)(2X - x0 + 1). But we also have a^2 term. So Δ = (X - x0)^2 - (X + 1)^2 + a^2.

Thus Δ = a^2 + (X - x0)^2 - (X + 1)^2. But we can also attempt to express a^2 in terms of x0 and maybe relate to something.

We have a^2 = 1 - x0^2. So

Δ = 1 - x0^2 + (X - x0)^2 - (X + 1)^2.

Now expand (X - x0)^2 = X^2 - 2 X x0 + x0^2. Then

Let's verify carefully:

Δ = 1 - x0^2 + (X - x0)^2 - (X + 1)^2.

Compute (X - x0)^2 = X^2 - 2 X x0 + x0^2.
Compute (X + 1)^2 = X^2 + 2X + 1.
Then (X - x0)^2 - (X + 1)^2 = (X^2 - 2 X x0 + x0^2) - (X^2 + 2X + 1) = -2 X x0 + x0^2 - 2X - 1.
Then Δ = 1 - x0^2 + (-2 X x0 + x0^2 - 2X - 1) = 1 - x0^2 -2 X x0 + x0^2 - 2X - 1 = -2 X x0 - 2X = -2X (x0 + 1).

So indeed Δ = -2 X (x0 + 1). That's neat. Then Y = Δ / (2a) = - X (x0 + 1) / a.

| (A + t v) - M |^2 = r^2 = 1. Since M = (0,0). So |A + t v|^2 = 1. But |A|^2 = 1 because A ∈ Ω. So

|A + t v|^2 = |A|^2 + 2 t (A·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = 1 + 2 t (A·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = 1.

Thus 2 t (A·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 → t (2 (A·v) + t |v|^2) = 0. So t=0 (A) or t = -2 (A·v) / |v|^2. So t_E = -2 (A·v) / |v|^2. Similarly, for Γ with center N = (d,0), radius R. The equation: | (A + t v) - N |^2 = R^2. Since A ∈ Γ, |A - N|^2 = R^2. So

Line2: y = 1(x - 0.817) - 1.183 = x - 0.817 - 1.183 = x - 2.0.

So det = √3. That's nice! So determinant = √3.

Then |v|^2 = 6 + 3√3. So distance squared from O to L = det^2 / |v|^2 = ( (√3)^2 ) / (6+3√3) = 3 / (3(2+√3)) = 1 / (2+√3). Simplify 1/(2+√3). Multiply numerator and denominator by (2-√3): (2-√3)/(4-3) = 2-√3. So distance squared = 2 - √3, which is exactly the radius squared. Therefore L is tangent to circle (BEF). QED.

Let's recall: In projective geometry, the set of circles passing through two points is a linear pencil. If we have two circles C1 and C2 that intersect at E and F, then any circle passing through E and F is a linear combination (with real coefficients) of C1 and C2. For example, take the circle with diameter EF (which passes through E and F) and the line EF (which is a degenerate circle, but not a circle). However the line is not a circle; but we can consider the degenerate circle (a line) as a circle with infinite radius, but in the equation form, a line is a degenerate case of a circle when the quadratic term is zero. But we want to express circles through E and F as: (x - e_x)^2 + (y - e_y)^2 + λ * L(x,y) = 0, where L(x,y)=0 is the equation of line EF, but then the resulting quadratic may have a nonzero quadratic term, so it's a circle (provided the coefficient of x^2 and y^2 are equal). However adding a multiple of a linear polynomial to a quadratic polynomial yields another quadratic polynomial. For it to be a circle, the coefficients of x^2 and y^2 must be equal. If we start with a circle equation like (x - a)^2 + (y - b)^2 + F = 0, which expands to x^2 + y^2 - 2a x - 2b y + (a^2 + b^2 + F) = 0. Then adding λ * (linear polynomial) = λ * (Lx + My + N) yields a new equation: x^2 + y^2 + (-2a + λ L) x + (-2b + λ M) y + (a^2 + b^2 + F + λ N) = 0. The coefficients of x^2 and y^2 remain 1, so it's still a circle (provided that the linear terms are not causing some degenerate condition). So indeed, if we have a circle passing through E and F, we can add λ times the line EF equation to get another circle passing through E and F? Actually does adding λ times the line equation preserve that the resulting equation is a circle? Yes, because the quadratic terms remain x^2 + y^2. But does the resulting equation represent a circle that passes through E and F? If the original circle passes through E and F, then at those points, the original equation equals 0. Adding λ times the line equation (which is zero on the line EF) will not change the value at points on EF, because if a point is on EF, then L(x,y)=0, so the added term is zero. So we could compute P as the point on the vertical line X such that the distances to A and C are equal. That's what we did. So P = (X, Y). And we have Y = - (c+1) X / s. That's an expression.

Let's try a different coordinate: Place MN as the x-axis, but also place the common chord AB as the y-axis (vertical). That is natural: Let AB be vertical. Then MN is horizontal. The midpoint O of AB lies on MN, so O is a point on x-axis. So we can set O = (t, 0). Then M = ( -a, 0) and N = (b, 0) with a > 0, b > 0, and t is some value (could be negative or positive). The distance between M and N is d = a + b. The radii: r = distance from M to A, R = distance from N to A. Since A is (t, s) with s > 0, B = (t, -s). Then we have:

M = (-a, 0), N = (b, 0). Then MA^2 = (t + a)^2 + s^2 = r^2. NA^2 = (t - b)^2 + s^2 = R^2.

Also, the line MN (x-axis) intersects Ω at C and Γ at D with C, M, N, D in that order. Since M is at (-a,0) and N at (b,0), with a,b>0. The order C, M, N, D: C is left of M, D is right of N. So C is at x = -a - r (since Ω radius r). D is at x = b + R (since Γ radius R). So C = (-a - r, 0), D = (b + R, 0). That's consistent.

Now, A = (t, s). Then P is circumcenter of ACD. Since C and D are on x-axis, P lies on vertical line at x = (C_x + D_x)/2 = ( -a - r + b + R )/2 = (b - a + R - r)/2. That's not as simple as before.

But maybe we can choose the coordinate such that the midpoint of MN is at origin. That gave P_x = (R - r)/2. That's simpler. So I'll stick with that. Let's set M = (-d/2, 0), N = (d/2, 0). Then C = (-d/2 - r, 0), D = (d/2 + R, 0). Then P_x = (R - r)/2. That's independent of d. That's nice. So P_x is expressed solely in terms of radii. That seems simpler because then P_x is a constant. That's different. So which is correct? The problem defines Ω and Γ with centres M and N, and line MN intersects Ω at C and Γ at D such that C,M,N,D lie in that order. Now, we also have A is an intersection point. We can param A as (x_A, y_A). Since A is on both circles, we have MA^2 = r^2, NA^2 = R^2. Also, A is not on MN. We can derive a relation between x_A and y_A.

Let’s denote:

M = (-d/2, 0), N = (d/2, 0). Then

MA^2 = (x_A + d/2)^2 + y_A^2 = r^2.
NA^2 = (x_A - d/2)^2 + y_A^2 = R^2.

Subtract: (x_A - d/2)^2 - (x_A + d/2)^2 = R^2 - r^2 → (x_A^2 - d x_A + d^2/4) - (x_A^2 + d x_A + d^2/4) = -2 d x_A = R^2 - r^2 → x_A = (r^2 - R^2) / (2d). Since r < R, x_A is negative. So A_x = (r^2 - R^2) / (2d). That's a negative value. Also, y_A^2 = r^2 - (x_A + d/2)^2.

Now, B is the reflection of A across MN (x-axis), so B = (x_A, -y_A). So B is (x_A, -y_A).

Now, we need to compute H, the orthocenter of triangle PMN. M = (-d/2, 0), N = (d/2, 0). So MN is horizontal. The altitude from P is vertical line x = P_x. So H has x-coordinate P_x = (R - r)/2. H_y is determined by (H - M) ⟂ (P - N). Let's compute.

M = (-d/2, 0). P = (X, Y) where X = (R - r)/2.

Then vector P - N = (X - d/2, Y). N = (d/2, 0). The condition (H - M) · (P - N) = 0. Since H = (X, h). Then H - M = (X + d/2, h). Dot with (X - d/2, Y) = 0 → (X + d/2)*(X - d/2) + h Y = 0 → (X^2 - (d/2)^2) + h Y = 0 → h Y = (d^2/4) - X^2. So h = (d^2/4 - X^2) / Y, provided Y ≠ 0.

-2X x_A + x_A^2 - 2Y y_A + y_A^2 = 2X (d/2 + r) + (d/2 + r)^2.

(A - N)·v = (c - d)(X - c) + s(Y - s) = (c - d)X - (c - d)c + sY - s^2 = (c - d)X - c^2 + cd + sY - s^2.

(A - N)·v = (c - d)X - (c^2 + s^2) + cd - (c+1)X = (c - d)X - 1 + cd - (c+1)X = combine X terms: (c - d - c - 1)X = (-d - 1)X = -(d+1)X. So

(A - N)·v = - (d+1) X + cd - 1.

Thus t_F = -2 ( - (d+1) X + cd - 1 ) / |v|^2 = 2 ( (d+1) X - cd + 1 ) / |v|^2.

## Solution
We prove the statement using analytic geometry.  After a suitable similarity transformation we may place the configuration in a convenient coordinate system.

---
### 1.  Choice of coordinates

Let \(M=(0,0)\) and \(N=(d,0)\) with \(d>0\).  Scale the whole figure so that the radius of \(\Omega\) becomes \(1\).  Because \(\Omega\) and \(\Gamma\) intersect in two distinct points, we have  

\[
R > 1,\qquad (R-1)^2 < d^2 < (R+1)^2,
\]

where \(R\) is the radius of \(\Gamma\).  Denote the common chord by \(AB\); it is perpendicular to \(MN\) (the line of centres).  Let  

\[
A=(c,s),\qquad B=(c,-s),
\]

with \(s>0\).  The condition that \(A\) lies on both circles gives  

\[
c^2+s^2 = 1 \tag{1}
\]  

\[
(c-d)^2+s^2 = R^2 \tag{2}
\]

From (2) we obtain  

\[
R^2 = d^2 + 1 - 2cd. \tag{3}
\]

The line \(MN\) (the \(x\)-axis) meets \(\Omega\) at \(C\) and \(\Gamma\) at \(D\) such that \(C,M,N,D\) lie in that order.  Hence  

\[
C = (-1,0),\qquad D = (d+R,0). \tag{4}
\]

---
### 2.  The circumcentre \(P\) of \(\triangle ACD\)

Since \(C\) and \(D\) lie on the \(x\)-axis, the perpendicular bisector of \(CD\) is vertical.  The midpoint of \(CD\) is  

\[
\frac{C_x + D_x}{2} = \frac{-1 + d + R}{2} .
\]

Therefore  

\[
P_x = \frac{d+R-1}{2} \;=: X. \tag{5}
\]

The point \(P\) also lies on the perpendicular bisector of \(AC\); using \(PA = PC\) we find the \(y\)-coordinate.

\[
PA^2 = PC^2 \;\Longrightarrow\; (X-c)^2 + (Y-s)^2 = (X+1)^2 + Y^2 .
\]

Expanding and simplifying gives  

\[
-2cX -2sY + (c^2+s^2) = 2X+1 .
\]

Because \(c^2+s^2=1\) and \(-2cX -2sY = -2(cX+sY)\), we obtain  

\[
-2(cX+sY) + 1 = 2X+1 \;\Longrightarrow\; -2(cX+sY) = 2X \;\Longrightarrow\; (c+1)X + sY = 0 .
\]

Thus  

\[
Y = -\frac{(c+1)X}{s}. \tag{6}
\]

So  

\[
P = (X,Y),\qquad Y = -\frac{(c+1)X}{s}.
\]

---

### 3.  The orthocentre \(H\) of \(\triangle PMN\)

\(M=(0,0)\), \(N=(d,0)\) are on the \(x\)-axis; therefore the altitude from \(P\) is the vertical line \(x = X\).  Consequently \(H\) has the same \(x\)-coordinate:

\[
H = (X, h).
\]

The condition that \(MH \perp PN\) (or \(NH \perp PM\)) determines \(h\).  Using \(MH \perp PN\):

\[
(H-M)\cdot(P-N) = 0 \;\Longrightarrow\; (X, h)\cdot (X-d, Y) = 0 .
\]

This yields  

\[
X(X-d) + hY = 0 \;\Longrightarrow\; hY = X(d-X) .
\]

Hence  

\[
h = \frac{X(d-X)}{Y}, \qquad Y \neq 0. \tag{7}
\]

Substituting \(Y\) from (6) gives  

\[
h = \frac{X(d-X)}{-\dfrac{(c+1)X}{s}} = -\frac{s(d-X)}{c+1}. \tag{8}
\]

Thus  

\[
H = \Bigl( X,\; -\frac{s(d-X)}{c+1} \Bigr).
\]

---
### 4.  The points \(E\) and \(F\) – second intersections of \(AP\) with the circles

The line \(AP\) has direction vector  

\[
\mathbf v = P-A = (X-c,\; Y-s). \tag{9}
\]

Because \(A\) lies on \(\Omega\), the second intersection \(E\) of \(AP\) with \(\Omega\) is obtained by solving  

\[
|A + t\mathbf v|^2 = 1 .
\]

Using \(|A|^2=1\) we obtain  

\[
2t\,(A\!\cdot\!\mathbf v) + t^2|\mathbf v|^2 = 0 .
\]

Thus \(t=0\) (point \(A\)) or  

\[
t_E = -\frac{2\,(A\!\cdot\!\mathbf v)}{|\mathbf v|^2}. \tag{10}
\]

A similar computation for \(\Gamma\) (with centre \(N\)) gives  

\[
|A + t\mathbf v - N|^2 = R^2 .
\]

Because \(|A-N|^2 = R^2\), we get  

\[
2t\,((A-N)\!\cdot\!\mathbf v) + t^2|\mathbf v|^2 = 0 .
\]

Hence  

\[
t_F = -\frac{2\,((A-N)\!\cdot\!\mathbf v)}{|\mathbf v|^2}. \tag{11}
\]

Now we compute the needed dot products.

First,  

\[
A\!\cdot\!\mathbf v = A\!\cdot\!(P-A) = A\!\cdot\!P - |A|^2 = A\!\cdot\!P - 1.
\]

\[
A\!\cdot\!P = cX + sY.
\]

Using (6) we have \(sY = -(c+1)X\); therefore  

\[
A\!\cdot\!P = cX - (c+1)X = -X .
\]

Consequently  

\[
A\!\cdot\!\mathbf v = -X - 1 = -(X+1). \tag{12}
\]

Second,  

\[
(A-N)\!\cdot\!\mathbf v = (c-d,\, s)\!\cdot\!(X-c,\; Y-s) = (c-d)(X-c) + s(Y-s).
\]

Expanding:

\[
(c-d)(X-c) = (c-d)X - c(c-d) = (c-d)X - c^2 + cd .
\]

\[
s(Y-s) = sY - s^2.
\]

Because \(sY = -(c+1)X\) and \(c^2+s^2=1\), we obtain  

\[
\begin{aligned}
(A-N)\!\cdot\!\mathbf v &= (c-d)X - (c^2+s^2) + cd - (c+1)X \\
&= (c-d)X - 1 + cd - (c+1)X \\
&= (c-d - c -1)X + cd - 1 \\
&= (-d-1)X + cd - 1 = -(d+1)X + cd - 1 .
\end{aligned}
\]

Thus  

\[
(A-N)\!\cdot\!\mathbf v = cd - 1 - (d+1)X. \tag{13}
\]

Now we also need \(|\mathbf v|^2\).  From the definition of \(\mathbf v\) we have  

\[
|\mathbf v|^2 = (X-c)^2 + (Y-s)^2 .
\]

But a simpler expression can be obtained from \(A\!\cdot\!\mathbf v\) and the relation \(A\!\cdot\!P = -X\):

\[
\begin{aligned}
|\mathbf v|^2 &= |P-A|^2 = |P|^2 + |A|^2 - 2A\!\cdot\!P = (X^2+Y^2) + 1 - 2(-X) \\
&= X^2 + Y^2 + 2X + 1 .
\end{aligned}
\]

Using \(Y = -\dfrac{(c+1)X}{s}\) we get  

\[
Y^2 = \frac{(c+1)^2 X^2}{s^2} = \frac{(c+1)^2 X^2}{1-c^2} = \frac{(c+1)X^2}{1-c}.
\]

Hence  

\[
X^2 + Y^2 = X^2\left(1 + \frac{c+1}{1-c}\right) = X^2\,\frac{2}{1-c}.
\]

Therefore  

\[
|\mathbf v|^2 = \frac{2X^2}{1-c} + 2X + 1. \tag{14}
\]

---

### 5.  The circumcircle of \(\triangle BEF\)

We have  

\[
B = (c,-s),\qquad E = A + t_E\mathbf v,\qquad F = A + t_F\mathbf v.
\]

Because \(|B|=|A|=1\) and \(A\in\Omega\), we know that the origin \(M\) lies on the perpendicular bisector of \(BE\).  Hence the perpendicular bisector of \(BE\) passes through \(M\) and is perpendicular to \(BE\).  Consequently the circumcentre \(O\) of \(\triangle BEF\) lies on that line; in particular \(O\) satisfies  

\[
O \cdot (E-B) = 0. \tag{15}
\]

Similarly, from \(|O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2\) we obtain  

\[
O \cdot (F-B) = \frac{|F|^2 - 1}{2}. \tag{16}
\]

Now we compute the vectors \(E-B\) and \(F-B\).

\[
\begin{aligned}
E-B &= (c + t_E v_x - c,\; s + t_E v_y - (-s)) = (t_E v_x,\; 2s + t_E v_y), \\
F-B &= (t_F v_x,\; 2s + t_F v_y).
\end{aligned}
\]

With \(t_E\) and \(t_F\) given by (10) and (11) we have  

\[
t_E = -\frac{2\,(A\!\cdot\!\mathbf v)}{|\mathbf v|^2} = \frac{2\,(X+1)}{|\mathbf v|^2}, \qquad
t_F = -\frac{2\,((A-N)\!\cdot\!\mathbf v)}{|\mathbf v|^2} = \frac{2\,(cd - 1 - (d+1)X)}{|\mathbf v|^2}.
\]

(The sign in \(t_F\) is correct because we have \(-\frac{2\,((A-N)\!\cdot\!\mathbf v)}{|\mathbf v|^2}\), and from (13) we get \(-\frac{2\,((A-N)\!\cdot\!\mathbf v)}{|\mathbf v|^2} = \frac{2\,(cd - 1 - (d+1)X)}{|\mathbf v|^2}\) – note that \(cd - 1 - (d+1)X\) is negative in our examples, so \(t_F\) is positive as expected.)

Now we look for a vector \(O\) satisfying (15) and (16).  Because \(|\mathbf v|^2\) is non‑zero and the two vectors \(E-B\) and \(F-B\) are not parallel, the system (15)–(16) determines \(O\) uniquely.  We can solve it by noting that (15) forces \(O\) to be orthogonal to \(E-B\); thus \(O\) is a scalar multiple of a vector perpendicular to \(E-B\).  A convenient perpendicular vector is  

\[
\mathbf p = \bigl( - (2s + t_E v_y),\; t_E v_x \bigr).
\]

Then \(O = \lambda \mathbf p\) for some \(\lambda\).  Plugging into (16) yields  

\[
\lambda\,(\mathbf p \cdot (F-B)) = \frac{|F|^2 - 1}{2}.
\]

Therefore  

\[
O = \frac{\frac{|F|^2 - 1}{2}}{\mathbf p \cdot (F-B)}\,\mathbf p. \tag{17}
\]

Now we compute \(|F|^2\).  From \(F = A + t_F \mathbf v\),

\[
|F|^2 = |A|^2 + 2t_F (A\!\cdot\!\mathbf v) + t_F^2 |\mathbf v|^2 = 1 + 2t_F (A\!\cdot\!\mathbf v) + t_F^2 |\mathbf v|^2.
\]

Because \(A\!\cdot\!\mathbf v = -(X+1)\) and \(t_F\) is given above, the expression simplifies.  But a more direct way is to use the fact that \(F\) lies on \(\Gamma\), so  

\[
|F-N|^2 = R^2.
\]

We have already used this to determine \(t_F\), so it is consistent.  To simplify the algebra we shall compute the needed quantities using the relations obtained so far and then verify the tangency condition.

Instead of solving for \(O\) explicitly, we can compute the distance from \(O\) to the line \(L\) through \(H\) parallel to \(\mathbf v\) and compare it with the radius \(r\) of the circle \((BEF)\).  The tangency condition is  

\[
\frac{|(O-H)\times\mathbf v|}{|\mathbf v|} = r. \tag{18}
\]

Because \(O\) is the circumcentre, \(r = |O-B|\).  So we need to show  

\[
|(O-H)\times\mathbf v| = |O-B|\,|\mathbf v|. \tag{19}
\]

Squaring both sides gives  

\[
\bigl((O-H)\times\mathbf v\bigr)^2 = |O-B|^2\,|\mathbf v|^2. \tag{20}
\]

Now we substitute the expressions we have for \(O\), \(H\), \(\mathbf v\), and then simplify using the relations (3), (5), (6), (14), and the dot‑product results.  The computation is lengthy but straightforward; we outline the key steps.

---

### 6.  Computing \(O\) and verifying the tangency

We first simplify the expression for \(\mathbf p\) and then compute \(\mathbf p\cdot(F-B)\).  

Recall  

\[
\mathbf v = (v_x, v_y),\quad v_x = X-c,\quad v_y = Y-s.
\]

Using (6) we have  

\[
Y = -\frac{(c+1)X}{s},\qquad v_y = -\frac{(c+1)X}{s} - s = -\frac{(c+1)X + s^2}{s}.
\]

Since \(s^2 = 1 - c^2\),

\[
(c+1)X + s^2 = (c+1)X + 1 - c^2 = (c+1)X + (1-c)(1+c) = (c+1)(X + 1 - c).
\]

Hence  

\[
v_y = -\frac{(c+1)(X+1-c)}{s}. \tag{21}
\]

Now,  

\[
t_E = \frac{2(X+1)}{|\mathbf v|^2},\qquad t_F = \frac{2(cd - 1 - (d+1)X)}{|\mathbf v|^2}.
\]

We also have  

\[
|\mathbf v|^2 = \frac{2X^2}{1-c} + 2X + 1. \tag{14}
\]

The vector \(E-B\) becomes  

\[
E-B = \bigl( t_E v_x,\; 2s + t_E v_y \bigr).
\]

A perpendicular vector \(\mathbf p\) (orthogonal to \(E-B\)) can be taken as  

\[
\mathbf p = \bigl( - (2s + t_E v_y),\; t_E v_x \bigr).
\]

Now we compute \(\mathbf p\cdot(F-B)\) where  

\[
F-B = \bigl( t_F v_x,\; 2s + t_F v_y \bigr).
\]

\[
\begin{aligned}
\mathbf p\cdot(F-B) &= -(2s + t_E v_y)\, t_F v_x + t_E v_x\, (2s + t_F v_y) \\
&= t_E v_x (2s + t_F v_y) - t_F v_x (2s + t_E v_y) \\
&= v_x \bigl( t_E(2s + t_F v_y) - t_F(2s + t_E v_y) \bigr) \\
&= v_x \bigl( 2s(t_E - t_F) + t_E t_F v_y - t_F t_E v_y \bigr) \\
&= v_x \bigl( 2s(t_E - t_F) \bigr).
\end{aligned}
\]

So  

\[
\mathbf p\cdot(F-B) = 2s\,v_x\,(t_E - t_F). \tag{22}
\]

Now we also compute the constant term on the right‑hand side of (16):

\[
\frac{|F|^2 - 1}{2}.
\]

We have  

\[
|F|^2 = 1 + 2t_F (A\!\cdot\!\mathbf v) + t_F^2 |\mathbf v|^2.
\]

Since \(A\!\cdot\!\mathbf v = -(X+1)\),

\[
|F|^2 - 1 = -2t_F (X+1) + t_F^2 |\mathbf v|^2.
\]

Thus  

\[
\frac{|F|^2 - 1}{2} = -\frac{t_F (X+1)}{|\mathbf v|^2} + \frac{t_F^2 |\mathbf v|^2}{2}. \tag{23}
\]

Now substitute \(t_F\) from (11) and note that \(|\mathbf v|^2\) appears.  We have  

\[
t_F = \frac{2(cd - 1 - (d+1)X)}{|\mathbf v|^2}.
\]

Then  

\[
-\frac{t_F (X+1)}{|\mathbf v|^2} = -\frac{2(cd - 1 - (d+1)X)}{|\mathbf v|^4} (X+1).
\]

And  

\[
\frac{t_F^2 |\mathbf v|^2}{2} = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{4(cd - 1 - (d+1)X)^2}{|\mathbf v|^4} \cdot |\mathbf v|^2 = \frac{2(cd - 1 - (d+1)X)^2}{|\mathbf v|^2}.
\]

So  

\[
\frac{|F|^2 - 1}{2} = -\frac{2(cd - 1 - (d+1)X)(X+1)}{|\mathbf v|^4} + \frac{2(cd - 1 - (d+1)X)^2}{|\mathbf v|^2}.
\]

We can factor \(2\) and simplify.  But perhaps it is more efficient to compute \(O\) directly using the linear equations in a different way.  Let’s go back to the equations:

\[
O\cdot u = 0,\qquad O\cdot v = k,
\]

where \(u = E-B\), \(v = F-B\), and \(k = \frac{|F|^2 - 1}{2}\).

Because \(u\) and \(v\) are not parallel, we can solve for \(O\) as  

\[
O = \frac{k}{\,u^\perp \cdot v\,}\, u^\perp,
\]

where \(u^\perp\) is any vector perpendicular to \(u\).  The choice \(u^\perp = \bigl( - (E_y - B_y),\; E_x - B_x \bigr)\) is convenient.  Then  

\[
u^\perp \cdot v = \bigl( -(2s + t_E v_y),\; t_E v_x \bigr) \cdot \bigl( t_F v_x,\; 2s + t_F v_y \bigr) 
= -(2s + t_E v_y) t_F v_x + t_E v_x (2s + t_F v_y) = 2s\,v_x\,(t_E - t_F)
\]

(as we already computed).  So  

\[
O = \frac{k}{2s\,v_x\,(t_E - t_F)}\, \bigl( - (2s + t_E v_y),\; t_E v_x \bigr). \tag{24}
\]

Now, we also have \(H\) and \(\mathbf v\).  The tangency condition (20) becomes  

\[
\bigl((O-H)\times\mathbf v\bigr)^2 = |O-B|^2\,|\mathbf v|^2.
\]

We can attempt to compute \(O-B\) and \(O-H\) using (24).  This looks messy, but perhaps we can find a more clever simplification: The circle \((BEF)\) may have a simple equation that is independent of the parameters.  Let’s compute the equation of the circle through \(B,E,F\) in terms of the parameters \(c,s,d\) and \(X\) by solving the linear system for the coefficients.

---

### 7.  Deriving the equation of \((BEF)\)

Write the general circle equation  

\[
x^2 + y^2 + Dx + Ey + F = 0.
\]

Plug in the three points:

\[
\begin{cases}
1 + Dc - Es + F = 0 &\quad\text{(B)} \\
1 + Dc + D t_E v_x + E s + E t_E v_y + F = 0 &\quad\text{(E)} \\
1 + Dc + D t_F v_x + E s + E t_F v_y + F = 0 &\quad\text{(F)}
\end{cases}
\]

(Recall \(A = (c,s)\) and \(E = A + t_E\mathbf v\), \(F = A + t_F\mathbf v\).)  Actually careful: \(E\) is a point; its coordinates are \((c + t_E v_x, s + t_E v_y)\).  So the equation for \(E\) becomes  

\[
(c + t_E v_x)^2 + (s + t_E v_y)^2 + D(c + t_E v_x) + E(s
